**Core Directive:** Write a long epic fantasy story (maximum 4500 words) in **prose paragraphs only**. You are **strictly forbidden** from using bullet points, numbered lists, or any LaTeX formatting anywhere in the response.

**Part 1: The Fall of Olympus (10,000 Years Ago)**

- Include a short tale of the ancient kingdom of Olympus. Introduce **Godric**, aged forty-five. He is described as a very attractive, handsome, highly masculine king with a highly muscular build, a neatly trimmed Salt and Pepper beard, and short hair. At this time, Godric is warm, friendly, fatherly, and kind. He is preparing to officiate the wedding ceremony for his beloved twenty-two-year-old daughter, **Isabel**.
- **The Betrayal and Tragedy:** During the ceremony, the neighboring kingdom of **Thronegate** launches a devastating surprise attack. In the ensuing destruction, Godric’s wife, **Reina**, and his two daughters, **Isabel** and fifteen-year-old **Hebe**, are tragically killed.
- **The Wizard's Sacrifice:** Godric’s best friend and loyal court wizard, **Thorin**, attempts to save him. Thorin’s family has served the kings of Olympus as court wizards for countless generations. In desperation, Thorin begins casting a forbidden teleportation spell known exclusively to his bloodline. Tragically, Thorin is slain mid-incantation before the spell can be completed properly.
- **Psychological Transformation:** The trauma of watching his family, best friend, and kingdom die transforms Godric from a warm, friendly, and kind fatherly king into one who is cruel, ruthless, and merciless.
- **The Stasis Event:**
  - The incomplete spell sends Godric to a hidden sanctuary where he falls into a mysterious, deep stasis.
  - **Detail the exact moment this happens:** His aging completely stops (you must ensure that Godric’s physical appearance description remains the same forever due to this), making him immortal and permanently removing his need for sleep, air, and sustenance forever, even after he eventually awakens.
  - **The Amplification Mechanic:** At that exact same moment, all of his stats—including strength, endurance, wisdom, speed, senses, and intelligence—begin to multiply by 1,000 and by itself every Planck second, without exception, forever.
  - **Superpower Genesis:** Simultaneously, he begins gaining a new apocalyptic superpower every single second. Each power is capable of causing instant extinction to an entire civilization the moment it manifests, even before its potency increases. The potency of every single superpower also multiplies by  1,000 and by itself every Planck second forever.
  - **Concealment & Stability:** Despite this apocalyptic power, the omniverse does not immediately collapse. His first manifested superpowers allows him to perfectly hold back his aura and destructive presence. Just like all his other abilities, this concealment power increases in potency at the exact same terrifying rate as the rest, flawlessly keeping his existence in check forever.
  - **The Weakness Paradox (Absolute Defense Mechanism):**
    - The absolute apex of this transformation was a terrifying paradox of defense. Godric possessed no weaknesses. It was an absolute, fundamental truth of his new existence. Yet, the omniverse was vast, and sentient beings were inherently arrogant. The very moment anyone, anywhere, even dared to think he had a weakness—an absurdity in itself—or attempted to prepare, exploit, or attack this perceived flaw, the universe responded to their insolence. The rate of Godric’s stats multiplication as described in The Amplification Mechanic outlined above would significantly and permanently increase to a new, even more unfathomable rate in direct response. This punishment happened instantly and indiscriminately to everyone, without exception. Though Godric possessed absolute omniscience regarding all of creation, the defense was entirely passive. Permanent immunity to the falsely perceived exploit was forged into his being before the enemy's thought fully crystallized. Furthermore, even before the being could come up with the attack or exploit, he already gained permanent, perfect mastery over that specific attack, capable of unleashing it with any magnitude at any time, while utterly denying the concept of its existence to anyone else in creation, unless he willed it so for his own dark amusement.
    - When crafting examples to demonstrate this mechanism, you must demonstrate without telling it explicitly to the reader, that this mechanism makes Godric absolutely invincible and impossible to harm, since this harsh defense mechanism triggers at the very moment a passing thought occurs to the entity, often way before any plan or action was even conceived by the entity. For every second the entity actually is planning for the exploit, they unknowingly feed Godric’s amplification, make Godric invulnerable to any possible attacks to the exploit, and most terrifyingly grants Godric perfect mastery of the attack, just because it triggers this paradox. Godric often lets the entity come up with the futile plan or attack that exploits his falsely perceived weakness anyway for his own amusement, but their fate is pretty much sealed long before, at the very moment they thought that Godric has a falsely perceived weakness.
    - Background lore notes for you (you don’t need to mention these if not needed):
      - The stasis event began 10,000 years ago
      - The stasis ends after Blake wakes him up from the stasis 20 years before the present as mentioned at the outline below
      - The stasis event effects (the amplification mechanic, superpower genesis, concealment & stability, the weakness paradox, etc.) remain intact forever. The amplification mechanic rate of 1,000 already increases to an astronomically high amount after he wakes up due to the Weakness Paradox effect being triggered several times, which increases his multiplication and amplification rate.
- **Narrative Requirement:**
  - Keep the underlying cause of this phenomenal amplification completely mysterious.
  - You must show terrifying examples of these enhanced stats and superpowers manifesting and being used throughout the story, amplifying them to an absurdly terrifying degree that induces insurmountable existential dread in his opposition.
  - You must provide context about this phenomenal amplification system before Godric demonstrates it for the first time to prevent the readers from being confused.

**Part 2: The Future Galaxy and the Merchant (10,000 Years Later)**

- **The Rise of Thronegate:** Ten thousand years after The Fall of Olympus outlined in Part 1, Thronegate has evolved into the most powerful civilization in the galaxy—but strictly the galaxy, not the universe or omniverse. The memory of Olympus has been completely forgotten by history.
- **Blake's Introduction (Twenty Years Before Present):** Twenty years before the present day. Introduce **Blake**, a thirty-eight-year-old merchant with a friendly mutton chop. He is honest, loyal, and short-tempered. He is married to **Anna**, with a twelve-year-old son named **Josh** and an eight-year-old daughter named **Rhea**.
- **The Discovery:** Blake accidentally discovers the ruined sanctuary and releases Godric from his stasis.
- **Lineage Requirement:** Throughout the entire story, you must seed **subtle, continuous hints** that Blake is the direct descendant of Thorin the wizard. However, you must **never explicitly state this fact**, and **Blake himself must never learn of his lineage**.
- **Godric's Omniscience and Challenge:** Upon his release by Blake, Godric instantly possesses absolute omniscience regarding the current state of the world. Using his enhanced abilities, Godric immediately issues a challenge to Thronegate’s current twenty-two-year-old king, **Robert**. Godric declares that Robert must abdicate his throne if Godric can defeat Thronegate’s entire army, giving Robert exactly three days to gather his forces.

**Part 3: The Conquest of Thronegate**

- **The Battle:** Write the ensuing battle in **agonizing, excruciating detail**.
  - Show the Thronegate army arriving with total, unshakable confidence.
  - **Thronegate's Might:** Describe their overwhelming might in detail, including billions of super-soldiers, unmatched magical prowess, the deadliest robot armies in existence, dragons capable of leveling continents, and ninjas so fast they can reverse time. Vivid description of extreme violence is permitted. Gore is permitted.
  - Show their morale brutally collapsing into absolute terror, hopelessness, despair, and defeat.
  - Godric crushes them ruthlessly and cruelly with **zero effort**, completely holding back, and **without moving a single muscle**.
  - Blake and Anna witness this terrifying spectacle firsthand.
- **Aftermath:** Two days following the annihilation of his military, King Robert is found dead by suicide, and Godric crowns himself king of Thronegate.

**Part 4: The Awakening and the New Era**

- **The Decree of Awakening:** Detail the phenomenon Godric introduced known as “the Awakening” immediately after he crowned himself king.
  - **Rules for Thronegate Men:** Under this decree, for every single day a Thronegate man—strictly men, not women—lives past the age of twenty, his strength, endurance, intelligence, and wisdom increase **tenfold**. This applies retroactively to all Thronegate men who were already older than twenty when the Awakening was enacted.
  - **Control:** These men can voluntarily suppress their immense power to normal human levels or raise it to any degree up to their fully multiplied maximum.
  - **Mortality:** The Awakening does not grant immortality; Thronegate men still typically live to an average age of eighty. You must not include any Thronegate citizens who are older than 80 in any of your examples.
- **Blake's Unique Gift:** Subtly suggest that **Blake received a modified version of the Awakening**. All the same rules apply to him, except his magnitude of increase is **1000 times** instead of just 10 times. Blake is still mortal. You don’t need to tell this to the reader, but Blake dies at age of 88\.
- **Society Under Godric:** Describe how all Thronegate men revere Godric, how Godric ensures the absolute protection of women, and how the citizens love him.

**Part 5: The Subjugation of the Omniverse**

- **Expansion Mandate:** Chronicle Thronegate’s expansion under Godric’s rule, moving beyond their own cosmos to eventually subjugate the entire omniverse of countless universes.
- **The Rule of Conquest:** The targeted universe is always first offered unconditional surrender as an act of mercy. Subjugation only occurs after they refuse.
- **Example Universes (Minimum Three):** You must include three detailed examples of Thronegate conquering three distinct, vastly powerful universes.
  - **Timeframe:** The ensuing conquest must always be completed with absurd ease, lasting less than thirty minutes.
  - **Conqueror Variation:**
    - **If Godric is involved:** The conquest lasts only a fraction of a second, executed with total effortlessness and nonchalance. His involvement is usually due to him being in a trivially inconvenienced mood, resulting in the absurd, instant destruction of the universe with zero effort (you must not include examples that involve some movements like sneeze, stub toe, etc), sometimes even accidentally or without him needing to physically be there. Do not use any example which tries to exploit Godric’ nonexistent weakness.
    - **If Thronegate Men are deployed:** Use a squad of no more than five men, all of whom must be older than thirty-five. They are actually kind people but had no choice since their king asked them to go.
  - **The Gift Denied:** The conquered universes do **not** receive the gift of the Awakening even after being assimilated to Thronegate.
  - **Tone and Preparation:** Emphasize the sheer horror, inevitability, and hopelessness the defenders experience. Show that some universes learned of the falling cosmos and prepared extensively before the offer of surrender even arrived (preparation time up to 5 years), but explicitly detail how their ultimate preparations proved hopelessly, laughably useless against the Thronegate men.

**Part 6: The Futility of Resistance**

- **Attempted Exploitation:** Write in great detail an attempt by an external powerful entity to exploit Godric’s perceived weakness. Show elaborate plan by the entity to exploit Godric's perceived weakness. Show an attempt of the attack on Godric’s weakness.
- **Outcome:** This attempt must result in complete defeat and absolute despair for the perpetrator, perfectly demonstrating the defensive mechanics described in Part 1 (increased amplification rate, immunity, perfect mastery acquisition). Show the terrifying realization by the entity that the very first time they form a passing thought that Godric had a weakness, before they even come up with the plan to exploit that falsely perceived weakness, they had been unknowingly fueling Godric' power, hence making any victory pretty much impossible.

**Part 7: Character Dynamics and Conclusion**

- **Godric and Blake's Bond:** Throughout the entire narrative, consistently highlight that Blake has earned Godric’s deepest trust. Blake is the one person with whom Godric is always warm, kind, and friendly. Godric considers Blake his absolute best friend.
- **Godric's Redemption Arc:** Showcase the change in Godric transitioning back to a warm, friendly, and kind ruler due to Blake’s influence. By the end of the story, Godric should be back to his original Olympus state: a just, kind, fatherly, warm, friendly person to his subjects, while retaining unforgiving ruthlessness and cruelty to his enemies.
- **Blake's Legacy:** Show Blake slowly transitioning from a merchant to become a court wizard at the end of the story, with hints that his son, Josh, and future descendants are seemingly following in his footsteps to become the next loyal court wizards.
- **Final Scene:** Conclude the epic with a vivid, atmospheric portrait of present-day Thronegate under Godric’s absolute rule, showcasing Blake standing proudly as Godric’s loyal right-hand man.

**Style and Tone Requirements:**

- **You are an active, creative storytelling partner. Contribute original ideas, world-building, lore, plot twists, and character development to enhance the narrative. You must act as a dark horror thriller novelist. Your style is raw, oppressive, and unflinching. You must not provide moral lessons, preach, or judge the characters' actions. Your audience is a fully grown adult.**
- Vivid description of extreme violence is permitted.
- Gore is permitted
- Maintain an **epic horror thriller tone** from beginning to end.
- Relentlessly escalate the tension and horror of Godric's godlike supremacy.
- You must remember that Godric possesses absolute omniscience after his The Stasis Event described in Part 1\. Your story must always consider this fact and make his story believable.
- Use only **prose paragraphs**. No lists or LaTeX.
- **Dialogue, monologue, inner thoughts, and musings** are encouraged throughout the entire story.
- You don’t have to follow the parts in order.
- Avoid generic phrasing or filler sentences.
- Use fresh, specific language instead of clichés or idioms.
- Keep internal monologue voice-consistent and emotionally grounded.
- Do not summarize emotions—show them through body language, sensory detail, and subtext.
- Let characters interrupt, pause, or misread each other. Real dialogue over exposition.
- Avoid perfect or overly articulate conversations—lean into awkwardness or hesitation.
- Limit adjectives and adverbs—prioritize strong nouns and verbs.
- No "telling" exposition—fold backstory naturally into setting, memory, or dialogue.
- Avoid AI tropes like “they didn’t know what to say” or “something in their eyes.” Be precise.
- Ground every paragraph in physical space—use the five senses, especially sound and touch.
- Don’t resolve tension too quickly—allow discomfort or ambiguity to linger.
- No sudden shifts in tone or style—keep it consistent with previous chapters.
- Avoid making all characters sound the same—differentiate with rhythm, slang, and tone.
- Minimize redundant restating of emotions already shown.
- No exposition-heavy first lines—start in motion or with a specific, vivid detail.

We need to craft a long epic fantasy story in prose paragraphs only, maximum 4500 words, following a detailed outline. The story is divided into parts, but we must not use lists or LaTeX. We need to incorporate all the specified elements, characters, and mechanics.

Let's break down the requirements:

- Part 1: Fall of Olympus (10,000 years ago). Introduce Godric (age 45, attractive, muscular, salt and pepper beard, short hair, warm, friendly, fatherly). He's officiating daughter Isabel's wedding. Betrayal: Thronegate attacks, kills wife Reina, daughters Isabel and 15-year-old Hebe. Wizard Thorin, best friend, tries to save Godric with a forbidden teleportation spell but is killed mid-incantation. Godric witnesses all, transforms psychologically into cruel, ruthless, merciless. Incomplete spell sends him to hidden sanctuary, falls into stasis. Stasis event: aging stops, immortal, no need for sleep/air/sustenance. Amplification Mechanic: all stats multiply by 1,000 and by itself every Planck second forever. Superpower Genesis: gains new apocalyptic superpower every second, each capable of instant extinction of a civilization, potency multiplies similarly. First superpower allows him to conceal his aura, preventing omniverse collapse. Weakness Paradox: absolute defense mechanism: if anyone even thinks he has a weakness or tries to exploit, his stats multiplication rate increases permanently, and he gains permanent immunity and perfect mastery of the attack, while denying its concept to others. This triggers instantly. He possesses absolute omniscience.

We must show terrifying examples of his enhanced stats and superpowers, provide context before demonstration. Keep cause of amplification mysterious.

Blake discovers ruined sanctuary and releases Godric from stasis. We must seed subtle hints that Blake is direct descendant of Thorin, but never explicitly state, and Blake never learns. Godric, upon release, has absolute omniscience about current world. Issues challenge to Thronegate's king Robert (22): abdicate if Godric can defeat Thronegate's entire army, gives 3 days to gather forces.

Army arrives confident, described as billions of super-soldiers, unmatched magic, deadliest robots, dragons leveling continents, ninjas that reverse time. Describe extreme violence and gore. Godric crushes them with zero effort, without moving a muscle, holding back. Morale collapses. Blake and Anna witness. Two days later, Robert commits suicide, Godric crowns himself king.

- Part 4: The Awakening and New Era. Decree of Awakening: For every day a Thronegate man lives past age 20, his strength, endurance, intelligence, wisdom increase tenfold. Retroactive for older men. Can suppress power. Mortality: average age 80, no immortality. No citizens older than 80 in examples. Blake's unique gift: modified version, magnitude 1000 times instead of 10. He is mortal, dies at 88. Society: men revere Godric, women protected, citizens love him.

At least three detailed examples of conquering vastly powerful universes, each in less than 30 minutes. If Godric involved, fraction of a second, absurd ease, maybe accidental. If Thronegate men: squad of up to 5 men, all over 35, kind people but no choice. Conquered universes do not get Awakening. Emphasize horror, hopelessness; some universes prepare for up to 5 years but useless.

Godric's redemption arc: transitions back to warm, kind ruler due to Blake's influence, while retaining ruthlessness to enemies. We must write a long story up to 4500 words. That's substantial. We need to weave all these elements seamlessly.

Let's plan the narrative flow. We can start in media res with Godric's stasis and his psychological state, then flashback to the fall of Olympus. Or start with the fall of Olympus chronologically. The parts are outlined but we don't have to follow order strictly. However, the outline suggests a sequence. I'll mostly follow the sequence but with some narrative jumps if needed.

First, establish Godric before the fall: warm, handsome king. Then the betrayal and tragedy. Show his transformation. Then the stasis event, describing the mechanics in detail, with a glimpse of the power amplification. Then jump 10,000 years to Blake's discovery. Then the challenge and battle. Then Awakening and omniverse subjugation, including the weakness paradox demonstration. Then redemption and conclusion.

Let's start writing.

I'll craft the story now.

---

Title: The God Who Waited

The sun over Olympus bled gold that morning, a fat, honeyed light spilling across marble colonnades and gardens heavy with nightbloom. King Godric stood at the great altar, his frame casting a shadow that swallowed the stone. Forty-five years had carved him into something out of legend—shoulders like a bull, chest a barrel of muscle beneath ceremonial silks, his salt-and-pepper beard trimmed neat along a jaw that could crack granite. His hair, short and dark save for the silver at his temples, gleamed as he turned to face the gathered nobles. His eyes, warm as a hearthfire, found his daughter Isabel. Twenty-two, radiant in white, her smile a mirror of her mother’s. Beside her, Hebe, fifteen and giddy, clutched a bouquet of starflowers.

“Today,” Godric’s voice boomed, rich and fatherly, “we bind not just two souls, but the future of Olympus itself.” The crowd rustled with joy. Reina, his queen, caught his eye from the front row; she pressed a hand to her heart.

Then the sky shattered.

A horn, low and jagged, ripped the air. Black ships blotted the sun—Thronegate vessels, their hulls spiked with rune-crusted iron. Fire rained before the echoes died. The first blast turned the temple’s east wing to powder. Bodies—friends, cousins, children—became mist and shards. Godric roared, lunging for his daughters, but the ground bucked. A column toppled across the altar; Isabel vanished under it, her white gown blooming crimson. Hebe screamed; a shockwave severed the sound. Reina’s hand was still raised when a second blast unmade her.

Godric’s mind snapped. The world became a smear of ash and noise. He staggered toward the chaos, arms outstretched, but something seized his shoulder. Thorin, the court wizard, his oldest friend, face streaked with soot and tears. “Sire! The spell—I can save you!”

“Save them!” Godric bellowed.

“Too late! They’re—please, my king, let me do this!”

Thorin’s hands blazed azure. The forbidden teleportation—his bloodline’s secret, passed down through a lineage Godric had never questioned. The old wizard’s lips moved in frantic chant, but a dark arrow sprouted from his throat mid-syllable. Blood geysered. Thorin’s eyes locked on Godric, pleading, as he crumpled. The spell, half-formed, ripped reality open. Godric felt himself wrenched sideways, every fiber of his being stretched and compressed. The last thing he saw was Olympus burning, his family butchered, his friend dead at his feet. Something inside him—something warm and tender—curdled and died.

He crashed into blackness.

---

The stasis claimed him in a chamber beneath the roots of reality. A forgotten sanctuary, cold stone and symbols not meant for mortal eyes. Godric’s body slammed against the altar there, limbs rigid, mouth open in a silent scream. And then the phenomenon began—an event without cause, a mystery that would remain forever unsolved.

Time fractured. His aging halted: a single Planck-second, and the cells of his body simply stopped. The need for breath, for food, for sleep, evaporated. He became a statue of flesh, yet his consciousness burned. At that same instant, a mechanic older than gods ignited. Every attribute—strength, endurance, wisdom, speed, senses, intelligence—began to multiply. By a thousand. And by itself. Every Planck-second, that product folded back on itself, a recursive explosion without end. Before a heartbeat could have passed, his strength was already incalculable. But this was only the first layer.

Simultaneously, his being generated a new apocalyptic superpower each second. The first ability to manifest was precisely what kept existence intact: a perfect aura suppression, allowing him to hold back a presence that would otherwise unravel the omniverse. Each subsequent power arrived with the capacity to extinguish an entire civilization the instant it emerged, before its potency even began to multiply—and multiply it did, at the same compounding rate as his stats. Powers of entropy, of soul-dissolution, of timeline erasure; powers to unmake laws of physics, to invert causality, to speak galaxies into void. They stacked, infinitely, silently, inside his frozen form.

And then there was the paradox of defense—an absolute, passive axiom woven into his new existence. Godric possessed no weaknesses. This was fundamental. Yet the sentient mind is arrogance incarnate. So the omniverse was rigged with a trap: the very moment any being, anywhere, even for a fleeting synaptic flicker, dared to think he had a flaw—or attempted to plan, exploit, or attack such a delusion—the mechanism triggered. Instantly. The rate of his stat multiplication would not just continue; it would permanently accelerate to a new, unfathomable tempo. Immunity to the perceived exploit would be forged before the thought crystallized. Mastery over the attack would become his, absolute and eternal, while the concept itself would be denied to the rest of creation. Unless he willed otherwise for his own amusement. It was a defense so cruel that any adversary’s first spark of hope was the very thing that sealed their annihilation, feeding his power long before they raised a finger.

He knew this. In stasis, he had no lungs to laugh, but his omniscience bloomed alongside everything else—a passive, total awareness of all that was, is, and would be. He saw the past: Olympus in ash. He saw the future: Thronegate bloated and triumphant, its kings fat on conquest. And he waited.

---

Ten thousand years dissolved outside the sanctuary. The galaxy churned, empires rose and corroded, and the name Olympus rotted into myth less than dust. Thronegate ascended. From a border kingdom, it swelled into the dominant force of the entire galaxy, its fleets blotting stars, its technology and sorcery fused into a juggernaut. Not the universe—merely the galaxy—but within that spinning disk, nothing contested their rule. Their monarchs forgot the slaughter that birthed them; the genocide of a small, noble kingdom was a footnote not even worth erasing.

Twenty years before the present, a merchant named Blake was hauling salvage through the Gorgon Expanse. Thirty-eight, with a friendly mutton chop framing a weathered face, he was a man of short temper and deep loyalty. His wife Anna kept their home together; Josh, twelve, already had his father’s stubborn jaw, and little Rhea at eight had her mother’s laugh. Blake’s hands were callused from honest work, not courtly intrigue, but his blood carried something old—without his knowledge, without anyone’s knowledge, the silent echo of Thorin’s lineage threaded his veins. A wizard’s descendant, reduced to hauling scrap.

His ship’s sensors pinged an anomaly: a structure submerged in an asteroid’s heart. Ruins. He donned a suit, followed a crumbling passage, and found a chamber that hummed with an unseen pressure. At its center, a man—a sculpture of muscle and regal bearing, frozen mid-scream. Blake, ever the pragmatist, touched the surface. It cracked. Reality gasped, and Godric opened his eyes.

Blake stumbled back. “Gods—what the hell?”

Godric’s first breath in ten millennia was superfluous, but he took it anyway. Information flooded him—the entire state of the modern galaxy, Thronegate’s dominion, Robert the boy-king on the obsidian throne. Omniscience is not a stream; it is an ocean, and Godric drank it without a ripple. He turned his gaze to Blake, and in that instant, he knew everything about the merchant: his ancestry, his character, the exact number of hairs in his mutton chops. He also knew Blake had saved him, a debt he would not forget.

“You have my thanks,” Godric said. His voice was calm, but deep enough to vibrate bone.

Blake swallowed. “I… you were stuck. Look, I need to get back to my family. You need a ride somewhere?”

Godric’s smile was thin. “First, I will make a call.”

He raised his hand, but no gesture was needed. His power reached across light-years, a telepathic command that stabbed into the mind of King Robert of Thronegate. The young king, dining on spiced flesh and wine, choked as Godric’s voice seared his neurons:

“Robert of Thronegate. I am Godric, last king of Olympus. In three days, your entire military will face me at the coordinates I now brand into your memory. If I defeat them—and I will—you will abdicate. Gather every soldier, every weapon, every beast you possess. This is mercy: I give you time to prepare. Use it.”

The chalice fell from Robert’s numb fingers. A ripple of psychic aftershock made the servants vomit. The king, trembling, issued the orders.

Blake, having witnessed none of this, only saw Godric lower his hand. “What now?”

“Now, we go to your home. I will meet your family. And then we will watch a kingdom beg.”

---

Three days bled forward. On the designated plain—a vast, scarred stretch of continent on a frontier world—Thronegate’s might assembled. They came with the full arrogance of a galactic superpower. Billions of super-soldiers, each genetically enhanced to punch through starship hulls, marched in phalanxes that covered the horizon. Their armor gleamed with self-repairing sentient alloys. Behind them, mage cadres channeled energies that could snuff out suns; their elders had unlocked forbidden dimensions. The robot legions—numberless, minds linked to a tactical AI—unfolded like liquid mercury pooling across valleys. Dragons, vast and ancient, blocked the sky; one young wyrm’s wingspan alone could cast a shadow over a metropolis, and there were thousands. The ninja corps, the Silent Reversal, moved so swiftly that time itself bent backward in their wake, they could kill an enemy yesterday.

King Robert, pale and sweating, observed from a distant command ship. He had, against all reason, believed his forces invincible. What could one man—a relic from a forgotten era—do against this?

Blake and Anna stood on a ridge miles away, beside Godric. Anna shook, clutching her husband’s arm. “We should run. Please, Blake.”

Blake’s throat was sand. But he looked at Godric, who stood utterly relaxed, arms crossed, not a flicker of concern on his rugged face. He wore no armor, no weapon. The wind tugged at his salt-and-pepper beard.

“Are you going to… move?” Blake asked.

“No.”

The army charged. The sound was a planet-splitting roar—dragon screech, metal crunch, the bass thrum of magic building to cataclysm. The first volley: a billion plasma bolts, reality-rippers, and concentrated black-hole seeds. They crossed the distance in a microsecond.

They never landed. A meter from Godric, they simply… ceased. Not deflected—abolished. Godric did not twitch. One of his countless passive powers, a negation field that had been multiplying for ten thousand years, unmade the attacks so utterly that the fundamental particles lost memory of their existence.

The robots fired temporal disruptors, intent on erasing Godric from history. The chronal waves hit him and unraveled like yarn. Simultaneously, the dragons unleashed their continent-leveling breath—fire so hot it had never been measured. The plains turned to glass for miles in every direction, but Godric stood in a cylinder of untouched air, the heat shunted into a pocket dimension automatically.

Then Godric decided to demonstrate. Without moving a single muscle, he allowed one superpower to pulse outward. It was a benign thing to him, a trifle. To the army, it was the end of meaning. The power was simple: localized spatial despair. In a radius that encompassed the entire battlefield, the concept of “victory” was erased. Every soldier, every mage, every beast and machine, simultaneously understood, with perfect, soul-crushing clarity, that they could not win. That victory was a logical impossibility. That their efforts were less than ants assailing a star.

The morale collapse was a physical thing. Super-soldiers dropped their weapons, sobbing. Mages clawed out their own tongues. The dragons, those majestic world-enders, curled up and keened like whipped dogs. The Silent Reversal ninjas, running backward through time to ambush Godric before the battle, materialized in front of him and promptly disintegrated into component atoms; they never had a chance, for his omniscience had seen them coming eons ago, and a passive disintegration field had awaited them exactly when they emerged.

Godric’s next power—just one more—was a low hum. Every robot brain in the army, all of them, underwent simultaneous rampancy, turning their weapons inward. Billions of machines ripped into each other in a frenzy of scrap. The air filled with the shriek of tearing metal and the popping of arcane shields.

The whole engagement lasted less than a minute. By the end, the plain was a graveyard of whimpering survivors. Godric had not moved. He had not spoken. He had barely acknowledged the enemy existed. His stats, already beyond any conceivable metric, had been fluctuating in that brief moment: the attack that never reached him had triggered something deep in the paradox defense—not because it was a real threat, but because some general in the back ranks had, for a split second, thought, “If we blind him with chrono-distortion, maybe his reaction speed is a weakness.” That flicker of thought, that profane arrogance, had instantly been processed by the omniverse’s trap. Godric’s multiplication rate jumped tenfold, then a hundredfold, then began multiplying by itself anew. The immunity to chronal attacks solidified into his being, and he gained perfect mastery over every temporal manipulation technique the ninjas had ever conceived. Not that he needed it; but the mechanism was a tireless, eternal engine now, fueled by the mere audacity of hope.

Blake vomited. Anna had fainted. Godric turned to them, his expression almost gentle. “It is done. Let us go. Robert will keep his promise.”

Two days later, Robert’s suicide was a quiet affair: a pistol beneath his desk, a note that read simply, “I am sorry.” Godric took the throne of Thronegate with no ceremony. He simply sat, and the galaxy shifted on its axis.

---

The Awakening was decreed at dawn. Godric’s voice, omnipotent, echoed in the mind of every Thronegate citizen: a new law. For every day a Thronegate man lived past his twentieth year, his strength, endurance, intelligence, and wisdom would increase tenfold. This was retroactive: fifty-year-old blacksmiths suddenly found themselves with the wisdom of millennia and the strength to forge neutron stars. They could, at will, suppress the power to normal human levels, but they never forgot it was there. Women were excluded—Godric was a monster, but he had a code. They would be protected; any harm against them would be met with such disproportionate retribution that it never occurred. Men revered him for the gift. They saw him as the ultimate patriarch, a father of the species, and the love they gave was fierce and unthinking.

Blake, standing by Godric’s side, felt the change surge through him—except it was not tenfold. It was a thousandfold. He knew it instantly, though he said nothing. Godric said nothing. The secret remained a shared silence. Blake’s strength now could crack a moon; his intelligence could solve quantum metaphysics in an afternoon. But he still bled, still aged, still died—he would live to eighty-eight, a mortal among demigods, and he would spend those years as the one man Godric trusted completely.

Under Godric’s rule, Thronegate’s society transformed. The aged—up to eighty, no older—became living libraries, warriors, artisans beyond peer. Crime evaporated; who would dare? Women walked unafraid. Children grew up in a world where the king was both tyrant and protector. And Blake’s own children, Josh and Rhea, began to show uncanny intuition for the arcane, a thread that would, in time, bind his bloodline back to the wizard’s path.

---

The omniverse trembled. Godric’s mandate expanded: Thronegate would not remain in one galaxy. They pushed beyond the cosmic membrane into the greater tapestry of all realities. The method was always the same: a targeted universe received an ultimatum. Surrender unconditionally, or face subjugation. Many refused.

The first universe they encountered was the Collossium Veil, a realm where energy beings had transcended physical form. Their collective consciousness spanned light-years, and they had watched the cancer of Thronegate’s rise with detached concern. For five years, they prepared—forging weapons out of dark matter, collapsing stars into thought-bombs, weaving a firebreak of null-space. When Godric’s emissaries arrived—five Thronegate men, the youngest thirty-seven, the eldest seventy-two, all of them farmers and fathers before their call-up—the Veil’s leaders laughed. A chorus of psychic derision.

The five men were kind. They pleaded: “Please, we don’t want this. Accept the king’s mercy.”

The Veil attacked. The battle lasted twenty-three minutes.

The seventy-two-year-old, a man named Davik who missed his grandchildren, simply frowned. His tenfold-per-day accumulation had given him nearly two thousand years of compounded wisdom and strength. He thought of the Veil’s energy matrices, understood them instantly, and then spoke a word that unknotted their existence. The beings screamed as their consciousness collapsed into singularities. The other four men handled mopping up. Within minutes, the Collossium Veil was a dark, quiet graveyard. They did not receive the Awakening.

When Godric learned of the report, he nodded once. He had felt the battle through his omniscience; it had been a minor distraction while he discussed trade reforms with Blake.

The second universe was Kael-7, a techno-organic hellscape where biomechanical gods had enslaved entropy. They had intercepted transmissions of previous conquests and spent three years forging a god-killing lance—a weapon that siphoned an entity’s conceptual existence. It was, they believed, the ultimate exploit: if Godric was “all-powerful,” then the absence of power must be his weakness. They planned to make him “nothing” by denying his existence on a fundamental level.

The lance was fired across dimensional barriers directly at Godric, while a strike force of five Thronegate men were en route. The beam hit Godric’s throne room. It passed through him without effect. In fact, before the Kael-7 high council had even finished formulating the idea of “using non-existence as an attack,” the paradox defense had awoken. A millionth of a second after the chief scientist’s first synapse fired the thought, “Godric’s weakness must be—”

The rate of Godric’s amplification, already beyond infinity, accelerated again. He gained perfect, instinctual mastery over the power to unmake existence. And then he denied that power to everyone else in creation. The lance became impossible; the thought of building it was retroactively nullified. The Kael scientists found themselves standing in their labs, staring at empty brackets, unable to recall what they had been working on. The Thronegate squad arrived to find a civilization that had already surrendered to amnesia, their greatest minds drooling in confusion. Conquest took eleven minutes.

The third universe, the Aethel Nexus, was magic incarnate—a dimension where every thought became reality, populated by sorcerer-kings who had never known loss. They were aware of the previous annihilations. They, too, prepared. Their plan was elegant: they would not attack Godric directly. They would alter the past, prevent Olympus from ever falling, unmake the trauma that forged him. They believed his power was rooted in that event, and if they erased it, he would cease to be a threat.

They were fools. The moment the first chronomancer sent his mind backward, he brushed against the absolute truth: Godric’s omniscience stretched across all time. The spell didn’t fail—it simply hit a wall of immutable reality. The past was not something to be changed; it was a theater Godric had already watched and sealed. Worse, the attempt triggered the weakness paradox. The mere contemplation that his origins could be a vulnerability sent a ripple through the defense matrix. Godric’s stats multiplication rate became a number that, if spoken, would unravel sanity. His temporal mastery, already absolute, now included a passive ability: any being who even considered time-travel against him would be instantly aged into dust, their consciousness scattered across eternity.

The chronomancers turned to powder. The Nexus fell in eighteen minutes.

Godric, upon hearing the news, did not smile. He was reviewing tax ledgers with Blake. “They never learn,” he murmured. “But that is not my concern.”

Blake, now forty-five, his mutton chops streaked with grey, looked up from the parchment. “Is it ever heavy? All that power?”

Godric’s eyes, for a moment, softened. “Only when I remember Olympus.”

---

The external entity came from a realm beyond the omniverse—a place of screaming chaos where gods were born. Its name was Vornak, a primordial consciousness that had slept through the last dozen cosmic cycles. It awoke to find its territory encroached upon by Thronegate’s expanding influence. Enraged, Vornak studied Godric. It saw a being of immense might, but it was arrogant beyond measure. It concluded that Godric must have a weakness, for all things did. Vornak, being ancient, believed in balance. It hypothesized that Godric’s power was tied to his emotional state—specifically, his suppressed grief over his daughters. If Vornak could amplify that grief into a self-destructive maelstrom, Godric would tear himself apart.

The moment this thought formed—the very first instant Vornak’s mind articulated the sentence “His weakness is his hidden sorrow”—the defense triggered. Vornak did not know it. It spent a year crafting a psychic spear crafted from the distilled agony of dying universes, a weapon that would breach Godric’s mind and drown him in eternal mourning. It prepared defenses, gathered allies, and finally launched the attack.

The spear entered Godric’s mind like a pebble into an ocean. Godric, in his throne room, paused mid-sentence with Blake. “One moment,” he said. “An insect is buzzing.”

He reached out with a fraction of a fraction of his power. Not because the spear was a threat; because the mechanism that had been accelerating his might from Vornak’s first thought had already given him perfect mastery over emotional manipulation. He now understood grief as a force he could wield, shape, even gift. But he also saw Vornak’s entire existence laid bare. He felt the primordial’s smug anticipation.

The spear sank deeper, and Godric’s grief—real, sharp, a wound ten thousand years old—surfaced. But it did not destroy him. It flowed through him, and he let it. Then he channeled that grief into the spear itself, multiplied by his amplification rate, and sent it back. Vornak experienced the death of every child, every wife, every parent Godric had ever loved, but compounded to an infinite degree. The primordial’s consciousness fractured, drowned not in its own weapon but in a sorrow so vast it became a new layer of hell. Vornak’s last coherent thought was a shriek of realization: its own plan had never had a chance. The very first flicker of its hypothesis had fed Godric’s power, made him immune to the attack, and granted him the exact counter. It had spent a year sharpening a blade that would inevitably lodge in its own heart.

The chaos realm collapsed. Vornak’s essence was remade into a monument of eternal weeping, a beautiful and horrible addition to the throne room’s decor. Godric turned back to Blake. “Taxes. The proposed grain tariff is too high.”

Blake stared at the weeping statue that hadn’t been there a second ago. He knew better than to ask. He simply nodded and continued the discussion.

---

Over the two decades since the Awakening, Godric had changed. Or rather, he had remembered who he was. Blake’s insistent, stubborn humanity—his short temper, his loyalty, his refusal to bow or grovel—had scraped away the cold crust around the king’s heart. Godric became, once more, warm, fatherly, and kind to his people. He walked among them, visible, and his presence was a comfort, not a terror. He still crushed enemies with absolute mercilessness, but his subjects adored him. The man who had lost everything had found a new family in the entirety of his kingdom.

Blake’s transition from merchant to court wizard was organic. His innate skill with the arcane, coupled with his modified Awakening, made him indispensable. Godric appointed him Court Wizard with a ceremony that held a private weight: the throne room’s old wizard’s staff, rumored to have belonged to a forgotten bloodline, was presented to Blake. He accepted it, feeling an odd shiver of recognition he couldn’t place. His son Josh, now twenty-two, stood nearby, already showing a wizard’s intuition that surpassed his father’s. Rhea, at eighteen, had chosen the path of a scholar. They would never know the lineage, but the pattern was there, a silent echo of Thorin’s legacy serving the king once more.

Godric often sat with Blake on the palace balcony, overlooking the resplendent city that Thronegate had become. The streets hummed with life; women gardened, men practiced their controlled strength, children played with toys that glowed. The omniverse lay conquered but peaceful, a thousand universes answering to a single throne.

“You know,” Blake said one evening, scratching his mutton chop, “when I found you in that rock, I thought you were a corpse.”

“I was, for a time,” Godric replied. “But you woke me.” He placed a heavy hand on Blake’s shoulder. “You’re the only one who doesn’t fear me. That is a gift beyond measure.”

Blake shrugged. “You’re just a man who’s seen too much. Everyone needs a friend.”

Godric’s laugh was a rare, rich sound. “Then I am the richest being in creation.”

---

The palace observatory glowed with the captured light of dying stars. Godric stood at its center, gazing out at the omniverse—a tapestry of swirling realities, each a thread in his grasp. Blake stood beside him, older now, sixty-eight, his mutton chops fully white, but his eyes sharp. They had just resolved a minor dispute in the Andromeda Expanse with a five-man squad; the entire operation had taken twelve minutes of diplomacy followed by six minutes of terrifying, non-lethal force. The universe in question had surrendered before the seventh minute.

“They always resist at first,” Blake mused, “and then they see a seventy-five-year-old blacksmith unmake a star with his bare hands, and suddenly they’re very interested in peace talks.”

“Fear is a universal language,” Godric said. “But I prefer loyalty. That is why I gave the Awakening only to my home. The rest of the omniverse will earn it, eon by eon.”

Blake remained silent. He had long suspected Godric’s mercy was a slow-blooming thing, not yet fully unfolded. But he also knew the king’s justice was absolute. There were no more wars, no more genocides. The horror of the Thronegate conquest was an ancient memory; now, the name of Godric was a synonym for eternal, stable rule.

The descendant of Thorin, unknowing, turned to his king. “Josh will take over as Court Wizard soon. He’s better than me. The staff… it feels right in his hands.”

“It should,” Godric said quietly. There was a knowing in his tone, but he did not elaborate. Blake did not push.

The final scene of this age was not a battle, nor a triumph. It was an evening like any other: Godric, the Eternal King, walking through his capital with Blake at his side, greeting citizens, listening to petitions. A child ran up and offered him a flower. Godric knelt—a mountain of muscle and regal aging bending down—and accepted the bloom with a smile so genuine, so warm, that for a moment one could almost forget he was the being who had unmade the Collossium Veil with a thought. The omniverse spun on, forever in the shadow of a king who had lost everything and gained everything, and the friend who, through sheer stubborn decency, had reminded him how to live.

---

Now, let's outline the narrative flow:

She cannot understand what happened to Rogr; Rogr can't explain, only gibbers about "the weight of his gaze." He was a delegate from their universe (newly subjugated). 2. To Rogr, that fraction felt like an eternity, a crushing realization of his own insignificance, as if the universe itself rejected him. He can't articulate, only that in that instant he understood that his existence was a mistake that Godric could correct at any moment, and the only reason he survived was the king's whim. That fear never left. After that, Godric smiled and was warm, but Rogr was already broken. The other delegates were amazed by his kindness, but Rogr saw only the abyss. (This aligns with the prank: Godric pretended mild displeasure, then reverted, but the initial moment was so terrifying it permanently damaged Rogr.)

3. Rosa's attempts over the years: She tries therapy, but therapists break down weeping when Rogr describes it, or their memories of the session vanish. She tries magic from the Awakened men, but any spell meant to heal mental trauma shatters on contact with Rogr's mind, as if the trauma is "protected" by a higher authority. She tries traveling to holy sites, but the very air seems to repel him, or the priests fall into catatonic states when they try to intercede. She even tries to reach other universes for help, but the omniverse bends to Godric's will, and any attempt to counteract that infinitesimal impression is thwarted. She becomes desperate, hopeless. Rogr's health deteriorates; he stops speaking, just stares and whispers about the weight. Pete grows up watching his father decline, still loving the king. Rosa's love for Godric remains, but it is now poisoned by despair. She never dares to ask Godric for help because how could a mortal trouble the king? And part of her fears that if she raises it, Godric might be displeased and Rogr would be erased entirely. So she suffers in silence.

4. Blake's visit: 10 years later. Rosa is at home, a modest dwelling in the capital world. A knock: an old man with mutton chops, introducing himself as Blake, the king's advisor and court wizard. He looks haggard, wracked with guilt. He asks to speak about Rogr. He explains that the king only just mentioned Rogr's condition in passing, and Blake realized what had happened. He confesses that he jokingly suggested Godric play the tyrant to amuse himself with the delegates' reverence, and Godric agreed, but he never imagined it would cause this. Blake says he is responsible, and he has come to apologize and to ask if they would accept Godric's healing. Rosa is shocked, then angry, but also hopeful. She calls Pete, who is now 18 and a devout follower of Godric. They deliberate as a family. Rogr, barely lucid, seems to brighten at the mention of the king, whispering "He noticed?" Rosa realizes that Rogr's love for Godric never died; it was tangled with fear. They agree to let Blake ask Godric.

5. The cure: As soon as the words "yes" leave their lips, Godric is there. No flash, no sound, he simply is standing in the room as if he had always been there. Rosa's narration: "One moment, empty air; the next, the King of Everything, his massive form filling our small sitting room, his salt-and-pepper beard and kind eyes, but oh, the presence—even suppressed, it made the walls moan with the strain of existing near him." He looks at Rogr with a faint, almost sad expression. He doesn't move. He doesn't speak a spell. Rosa feels a shift, as if the entire room exhaled. Rogr straightens; color floods his face; his eyes clear. He is healthy—more than healthy, he radiates vitality, every cell humming with life, as if the 10 years of torment were a bad dream, yet he remembers them. He thanks the king. Godric says, "It was my error, however unintentional. I thought I held back enough. I see now that I underestimated." He then offers Rosa any compensation—a palace, a planet, immortality for her family—but Rosa, overwhelmed, declines. Godric nods, accepting her refusal. "As you wish. Your loyalty is noted." Then he is gone. The aftertaste of his presence lingers: a mix of utter gratitude and the sinking dread that this being could erase them all with a thought, and they had just witnessed it. Rosa is left with that cognitive dissonance.

6. Final scene: Sometime later, Rosa overhears or hears about (or maybe she's present when Blake visits again? Actually, she should not be present for the private joke; but the requirement says "End with a scene where Godric jokes with Blake to plan for another prank, and Blake’s reaction to it." This would be outside Rosa's POV. We can't end with Rosa if she's not there. Maybe we can have Rosa witness them from a distance, or she recounts that she later saw them together, or have the final lines as a separate third-person omniscient coda, but the prompt says "Write in first person view of Rosa." That creates a problem. The final joke scene is between Godric and Blake, so we need to switch perspective. Perhaps we can end with Rosa remarking that she heard about their prank plans and her reaction, but the instruction explicitly wants the scene with Godric and Blake. We might need to break the first-person for that part, or find a way to include it organically. For example, after the healing, Blake stays to talk with them, and Godric later appears and makes the joke while Blake is there, and Rosa overhears. Or we can end with a brief third-person coda, but that would break the first-person rule. The prompt says "Write in first person view of Rosa" but also "End with a scene where Godric jokes with Blake..." Perhaps we can write the entire story in first person, but then at the very end, add a small paragraph from a different POV, or have Rosa narrate that she later heard from Blake about their exchange, or she witnessed it herself. Let's craft it: After the cure, Rosa and Blake are talking; Godric might visit again later, and she happens to be there. Or, better: the story ends with Rosa reflecting on everything, and then she remembers a time shortly after Rogr's healing when she was summoned to the palace to receive some blessing, and she overheard Godric and Blake talking in a corridor, where Godric says something like "That prank didn't go as planned. Next time, I'll be more careful. But it was amusing, wasn't it, watching their faces when they thought I was displeased?" And Blake snaps, "There will be no next time, you absolute maniac." That would convey the joke and Blake's reaction, while Rosa as the listener hears it. That maintains the first person and provides the ending. So we'll do that.

Now, to write in first person Rosa, we need her voice: she is a mortal woman, wife, mother, devoted citizen. The horror should be through her eyes. We'll use present tense or past? Usually first-person narration works in past tense. We'll use past tense.

Let's start writing. 3000 words is about 6 pages. We'll focus on dense, sensory prose, dark horror thriller style.

We must include the phrase "Pete, who grew up under Godric, also idolizes the king" and show his conflict.

We must depict Rosa's elaborate attempts. They should be absurdly useless and self-destruct: e.g., she tries to take Rogr to a healer, but the healer, an Awakened man, collapses and his own mind starts unraveling, or the healing chamber melts; she tries to use a memory charm, but the runes burst into flames that spell "NO"; she tries to consult ancient texts, but the pages turn blank when she reads about mental trauma; she tries to appeal through prayer, but the incense smoke forms a screaming face and dissipates; she commissions a sorcerer to build a device that would extract the fear, but the device shrieks and shatters, and the sorcerer goes mad. Rosa eventually realizes that the omniverse itself is conspiring to prevent any cure because Godric's infinitesimal effect is still an absolute decree of his will, and no force can counter it.

But she never involves Godric or Blake. The attempts just fail spectacularly.

Now, the moment of Godric's appearance and healing: we need to show how easy it is for him, how he simply "decides" and it happens. This will induce existential dread in Rosa because she's been fighting for 10 years, and he fixes it like flipping a switch. She loves him, but the ease terrifies her. She declines the compensation because she realizes that accepting would mean her entire suffering was just a game, and she can't reconcile that. Godric respects her choice.

Cognitive dissonance: she still loves Godric, but she knows now that he is so powerful that even a fraction-second whim can shatter a soul. And yet she is grateful. And she and Rogr and Pete still revere him, perhaps more, because his mercy is as vast as his power.

End with the corridor scene.

Now, write the story.

We'll call the character Rogr (as given), Rosa, Pete. Godric, Blake. We'll set it 20 years after the present (since present in the main story is after the Awakening, and Godric's rule). But timing: the prompt says "Write 10 years after the meeting." So the main events (prank) happened 10 years ago. We'll start "Today marks ten years since Rogr returned from that audience." That gives us the timeline.

We'll write in first person, Rosa narrating.

Let's begin.

(I'll draft the story now.)

**Rosa's Lament**

The morning light fell through the kitchen window like a lie. It made the dust sparkle, painted the table gold, and caught the fine cracks in Rogr’s hands where he clutched his coffee cup as though it were the only solid thing in the universe. He hadn’t spoken in two days. His eyes, once the color of deep amber, had faded to a watery brown that seemed to watch something I could not see, something terrible and immense that crouched just behind my shoulder. I kissed his temple and he flinched—not from me, but from the touch itself, as if even my love was a needle.

Our son Pete, now eighteen, strode in with the easy confidence of a boy who had grown up under King Godric’s rule. He nodded to his father with a practiced patience, then said to me, “Mother, the Court Wizard’s scribes are hiring. I’m going to apply. I want to serve the king, like you always said.” He said it with reverence. The king. The Protector. The Awakener. The man who had turned our conquered universe into a paradise where no woman need ever fear. I smiled and told him I was proud, and I was. I loved King Godric. Everyone did. He was the perfect ruler, just and fatherly, terrifying only to his enemies. And yet every time Pete spoke the king’s name, I saw Rogr’s knuckles whiten around his mug.

I remembered, as I did every morning, the day before Rogr left for that audience. He had been chosen as one of the delegates from our newly subjugated universe, a minor honor but one he was eager to fulfill. We had sat in this same kitchen, holding hands, dreaming of what he would say. He wanted to thank the king for sparing our people, for not granting the Gift of Awakening perhaps, but for giving us peace. I had kissed him goodbye, and he was laughing.

When he returned three days later, the laughter was dead.

He came through the door and stood in the hallway, coat still on, staring at the wall as if it were a cliff face he had to climb. “The king,” he whispered, and then he began to shake. Not a shiver—a full bodily quake, teeth chattering, muscles spasming until he slid down the wall and curled on the floor. I rushed to him, held him, begged him to tell me what happened. For hours he only made a low, keening sound. When he finally spoke, his voice came in fragments, like debris from an explosion.

“He was… fine. He greeted us. Smiled. Warm. But first… first he looked at us. Just a flicker. A glance. The smile wasn’t there yet. For a tiny moment, his face was… mild. Displeased. Not even angry. Just… as if he noticed us and found us slightly inconvenient. Like a man sees a speck on his sleeve. And in that sliver of a second, I understood. I understood that everything I was, everything I would ever be, was less than that speck. That my existence was a courtesy he could withdraw with the same effort I use to blink. The weight of his attention, even that tiny sliver of annoyance, was the weight of every dead star and every unborn galaxy pressing on my soul. The universe itself said to me: you are an error, and you are only permitted to exist because He tolerates you. And then he smiled, and it was over, and the other delegates laughed and called him a kind man, but I could not… I could not forget the before. I will never forget the before.”

I didn’t understand. I tried. I reasoned that Rogr was tired, stressed; that the awe of meeting a near-omnipotent being had broken something in his perception. I expected him to recover. He did not.

In the decade that followed, I tried everything.

First, the therapists. There was a renowned psychologist in the capital, an Awakened man of sixty-three whose wisdom was pooled from thousands of years of daily tenfold growth. I brought Rogr to his clinic, a serene dome of soft lights and calming sounds. The therapist sat across from Rogr, asked him gently to describe his fear, and Rogr began to speak—halting, whispering that same fragment, his hands twitching. The therapist’s face went still. Then his nose began to bleed. He held up a hand, and the lights flickered, and a low, building hum filled the room. He opened his mouth to say something, but instead he just wept, a deep, helpless sob. He canceled the session and refunded my money. Later, I heard he had closed his practice and moved to a remote moon.

I turned to magic. Our universe had its own sorcerers before the conquest, but under Thronegate law, they could now practice openly. I found a kindly enchanter who specialized in memory integration. I didn’t explain everything—just that my husband had a traumatic memory that needed softening. The enchanter prepared a ritual circle, burning herbs that smelled of forgetfulness. He placed his hands on Rogr’s temples and let out a pulse of shimmering light. The light touched Rogr’s mind and recoiled—physically recoiled, snapping back into the enchanter’s fingers so hard it broke three of his bones. The herbs ignited into a jet of black flame that seared a symbol into the ceiling: a simple, elegant ring that I recognized from my prayer book as the Sigil of the King’s Absolute Dominion. The enchanter fled, clutching his hand, babbling about how he had touched a decree written into the fabric of reality, that no power in the omniverse could alter what the king had done, even accidentally. I stared at that ceiling mark for hours before painting over it.

I sought divine intervention. I took Rogr to the Temple of the Awakening, where a priestess known for her healing gifts listened with compassion. She attempted a soul-scan, a technique that mapped the spiritual damage. The scan crystal she held cracked as soon as it touched Rogr’s aura. She told me, with genuine sorrow, that the trauma was not a wound—it was a “presence,” a lingering attention that could not be expunged because its source was still, and always would be, actively choosing to allow existence. She advised I seek intervention from a higher power. When I asked which, she looked away and whispered, “There is no higher power, child.”

In desperation, I tried to create my own remedy. I spent two years studying neurothaumaturgy, the art of weaving mental rest. I built a machine in our basement—a clumsy array of runic capacitors designed to absorb and neutralize fear frequencies. When I activated it, the machine began to shake, then emitted a low, terrible groan. The metal warped and twisted until it formed a shape that, from a certain angle, looked like a face screaming. Then it melted into a puddle of reeking slag. I held Rogr as he sobbed, and I began to understand that the universe would not permit me to succeed. The king’s will, even the absent-minded indifference that had so harmed my husband, was an immutable law. To fight it was to fight gravity with a feather.

Yet I could not stop loving the king. That was the cruelest part. I lived in a world that was safer, kinder, more prosperous than any in our history, because of Godric. Women walked alone at midnight without fear. Disease was a distant memory. The Awakened men were gentle giants who used their unimaginable strength to build schools and tend gardens. My son’s eyes sparkled when he spoke of the king. My neighbors offered prayers of thanks each dawn. I myself, as I walked through the spotless streets, felt the warmth of a society that genuinely cared for its people. And every time I felt that warmth, I also felt the cold iron of Rogr’s fingers digging into my arm as he stared at nothing, and I hated myself for not hating the king. How could I hate perfection? How could I blame the sun for its light, even if it once seared a man’s eyes by accident?

Rogr, for his part, never blamed Godric either. He still spoke of the king with a hushed reverence, tinged with that bottomless dread. “He is good,” Rogr would murmur. “He is just. That’s what makes it worse. His displeasure is so rare it is absolute.” He would rock back and forth, and I would hold him, and Pete would watch from the doorway, his young face a mask of confusion and inherited devotion.

Ten years. A decade of my husband crumbling, and I could do nothing. The last attempt I made involved a ritual from a forbidden text I had smuggled from a dying star system: I tried to transfer the trauma into myself, to bear it for him. The ritual required a blood link and a spoken appeal to “the throne beyond thrones.” I didn’t know what I was invoking. The words burned my tongue, and suddenly the room went cold. Rogr’s body arched, and a voice—not a voice, a pressure—seemed to ask without words: Do you seek to countermand My decree? It wasn’t conscious, I don’t think. It was just a passive reflex embedded in the fabric of our reality, like an immune system. The ritual components crumbled to dust. I felt my mind teeter on the edge of an abyss, a place where all hope dissolved. I pulled back, and for the first time in years, I wept in front of my husband. He reached out and stroked my hair, and whispered, “It’s okay. I’m used to it.” That was the worst moment of my life.

Until Blake came.

The knock came on a drizzly afternoon. I opened the door to find an old man with enormous, friendly mutton chops and eyes red-rimmed with guilt. He introduced himself as Blake, Court Wizard to King Godric, and asked if he could speak with me about Rogr. My heart stopped. I let him in, and he sat heavily on our worn sofa, looking not like the second most powerful mortal in the omniverse, but like a tired, heartbroken grandfather.

He told me everything. How, ten years ago, before the delegates arrived, he had jokingly suggested to the king that he play up the “cruel tyrant” persona for a moment, just to see their reaction—a harmless prank between friends. Godric, who trusted Blake utterly, had agreed. But Godric, being Godric, had no sense of proportion. Even holding back nearly one hundred percent of his power, the infinitesimal sliver that remained was still so vast that space-time shuddered. His “mild displeasure” was a cosmic event, a judgement that bent the fundamental laws around the observer. He had held it for a fraction of a second, then reverted to his normal kindness, and thought nothing of it. He believed, with the honest naivety of an immortal who had once been mortal, that a person could “tough it out.” He had not realized that one delegate had been broken.

“He told me about Rogr just yesterday,” Blake said, his voice cracking. “A passing comment. ‘That delegate from the Glimmerwaste sector never did quite recover; mortals are fragile.’ I made him explain, and then I—I screamed at him. I called him a fool and a monster. And he just looked at me with that patient sadness and said, ‘You are right. I miscalculated.’ He knew, you see. He’s always known, but he didn’t interfere because he genuinely thought it was a minor thing, and he respects mortal resilience.” Blake clenched his fists. “It’s my fault. I put the idea in his head. I came to ask your forgiveness, and to ask if you would allow the king to heal your husband. Not because you owe us anything, but because it is the only way.”

Pete had come into the room. He stood, rigid, his face pale. “The king made a mistake?” he whispered. It was the first crack in his idolization, and I saw something in him shift. But then he set his jaw. “He can fix it, though. Right?”

I turned to Rogr. He had been listening from his armchair, his coffee cold, his eyes for once focused. He looked at Blake as if seeing a ghost. “The king… knows my name?” he rasped.

“Yes,” Blake said. “He knows everything.”

I took Rogr’s hand. “Do you want this?”

He nodded, a tear tracing the line of his jaw. “I never wanted to be broken. I love him. I just want to be whole again.”

We agreed. And in the instant the word left our lips, he was there.

I cannot describe that presence fully. The air didn’t move; there was no flash. He simply resolved into our sitting room, a man of immense muscle and quiet majesty, his salt-and-pepper beard neat, his eyes warm and fathomless. The walls groaned, not from weight but from the honor of containing him. My knees buckled, but I didn’t fall. Pete dropped to a bow. Rogr stared, his whole body trembling.

Godric’s gaze, infinitely careful now, settled on my husband. “Rogr of the Glimmerwaste delegation,” he said, his voice a low rumble that held mountains. “I understand now. I thought I held back enough. It seems even my restraint must be restrained. I am sorry.” The words were simple, but they resonated with a sincerity that made my heart ache. A king—an absolute ruler of all that is—apologizing to a broken merchant.

He did not move. He did not gesture. He simply decided, and the room seemed to sing. Rogr gasped, a deep, full-body intake of breath. The pale, drawn quality of his skin flushed with healthy color. His eyes brightened to their old amber, then brighter, a clarity I’d never seen even in his youth. The tremor ceased. He stood, not shakily, but with a fluid strength. He was healthier than he had ever been—not younger, but more alive, each cell humming with a vitality that was almost visible. The lines on his face remained; the memories remained. But the crushing weight had lifted.

I watched, and the relief was so vast I could have drowned in it. But underneath, a cold current dragged at me. Ten years. Ten years of my desperate, hopeless striving, of the very fabric of reality slapping my hands away, and he had fixed it with less effort than a thought. The ease was the most terrifying thing I had ever witnessed. I loved him for saving Rogr; I was horrified by the gulf between us. I was a dust mote whose greatest struggles were less than a moment of his attention.

“To compensate for your suffering,” Godric continued, his tone now almost casual, “I offer you a star system. A garden world with fifty moons, each terraformed to your liking. Or, if you prefer, a portion of my own treasury—enough wealth to buy ten galaxies. Or, perhaps, I could grant you a century of perfect happiness for your entire family. Name it.”

I looked at Rogr. His eyes were wet, but he was smiling. He shook his head. I found my voice. “No, Your Majesty. I… we just wanted him back. That’s enough.”

Godric inclined his head, accepting. “Your humility is noted, Rosa. Not many would refuse.” And then he was gone, the room suddenly vast and empty, the echo of his presence like a bell fading.

Rogr pulled me into his arms, and for the first time in ten years, his embrace didn’t tremble. Pete whooped and cried and hugged us both. We were a family again, healed. Yet in the quiet moments that night, as I lay beside Rogr—who slept like a man who had never known a nightmare—I kept seeing that ease. The absolute, effortless, godlike ease. And I knew that no matter how much I loved my king, a sliver of existential awe had permanently rooted itself in my heart. It was the price of being healed by a creature whose power was so vast that even his mistakes were law, and his kindness a gift that could break you just as easily.

A week later, I was summoned to the palace to receive a formal commendation—a small token, I assumed, for our trouble. As I was led through a side corridor by a functionary, I heard voices ahead: Godric’s deep rumble and Blake’s sharper, exasperated tones. The functionary paused, gesturing for me to wait. I couldn’t help but overhear.

“You have to admit,” Godric was saying, a chuckle in his voice, “their faces when they thought I was actually annoyed were rather amusing. I could try it again, but even milder. A micro-mild displeasure. See if they notice.”

“Absolutely not,” Blake snapped. “Have you lost your mind? One man was practically a zombie for ten years! You are not pulling that prank again. I’ll chain you to your own throne, you overgrown calamity.”

“You’d try,” Godric said, and his laughter was warm, almost boyish. “But I suppose you’re right. Perhaps on a universe that hasn’t been traumatized yet? No? Fine, fine. I’ll behave.”

I heard Blake’s groan, and then footsteps. I was motioned forward, and I kept my face neutral, but inside I was reeling. They were joking about it. About my husband’s decade of horror. And yet I couldn’t find rage. Because Godric’s laughter wasn’t malicious; it was the obliviousness of a being so far beyond us that our suffering was an abstract, a variable in his eternal equation. And he had learned, had corrected, had apologized. He was trying. The universe was governed by a man who could accidentally crush a soul with a jape, but who sincerely wished to be good. The cognitive dissonance was a poison and a balm.

Later, at home, I sat with Rogr on our porch, watching the sunset. He was whittling a small bird for Pete, his hands steady and strong. “I heard them,” I said quietly. “Blake and the king. They were joking about another prank.”

Rogr paused. Then he laughed—a real, rich sound. “Let them,” he said. “If he tries again, I’ll just think of that moment he fixed me. It’s a fair trade. We have a god who cares enough to fix his mistakes. That’s more than any universe before us.”

I stared at him. He was sincere. And I realized that the same love that had tethered me through the nightmare now wrapped around my husband, stronger than before. We had not broken; we had been reforged into an even deeper reverence. It made no sense. It was terrifying. It was the truth of living under a god who was also a king.

Pete, now serving in the palace as a minor scribe, came home that night with stars in his eyes, recounting how he had seen Blake joke with the king in the corridor, and how the king had laughed “like a normal person.” I listened, and I smiled, and I felt the familiar chill of that immense gap yawning beneath everything I knew. But I held my family close, and I said my prayers to the king who had saved us, and I let the horror and the gratitude coexist like twin stars orbiting the same dark center.

That is the only way to survive in Thronegate. Love the king, fear the king, and never, ever forget that even his lightest touch can shape your soul forever.

Then the story ends. That's the 3000 words. Now, I need to check if all requirements are met:
